</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Biodiesel</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">"Green diesel" redirects here. For dyed fuel, see <a href="/wiki/Fuel_dyes" title="Fuel dyes">fuel dyes</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about transesterified lipids. For hydrogenated alkane renewable diesel, see <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">Vegetable oil refining</a>. For biomass and organic waste-to-fuel production, see <a href="/wiki/Biomass_to_liquid" title="Biomass to liquid">Biomass to liquid</a>. For unmodified vegetable oil used as motor fuel, see <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">Vegetable oil fuel</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For a broader coverage related to this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/220px-Soybeanbus.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/330px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/440px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="322" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bus run by biodiesel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Methyl_Linoleate.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Methyl_Linoleate.png/170px-Methyl_Linoleate.png" width="170" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Methyl_Linoleate.png/255px-Methyl_Linoleate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Methyl_Linoleate.png/340px-Methyl_Linoleate.png 2x" data-file-width="796" data-file-height="1359" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Methyl_Linoleate.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Space-filling model of methyl linoleate, or linoleic acid methyl ester, a common methyl ester produced from soybean or canola oil and methanol</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethyl_Stearate.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ethyl_Stearate.png/220px-Ethyl_Stearate.png" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ethyl_Stearate.png/330px-Ethyl_Stearate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ethyl_Stearate.png/440px-Ethyl_Stearate.png 2x" data-file-width="1790" data-file-height="763" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethyl_Stearate.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Space-filling model of ethyl stearate, or stearic acid ethyl ester, an ethyl ester produced from soybean or canola oil and ethanol</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Biodiesel</b> refers to a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a> – or animal fat-based <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a> consisting of long-chain <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> (<a href="/wiki/Methyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Methyl">methyl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_group" title="Ethyl group">ethyl</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Propyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Propyl">propyl</a>) <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>. Biodiesel is typically made by chemically reacting <a href="/wiki/Lipids" class="mw-redirect" title="Lipids">lipids</a> (e.g., vegetable oil, soybean oil,<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup> animal fat (<a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a><sup id="cite_ref-NRA1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRA1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AusBiofuels_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AusBiofuels-3">[3]</a></sup>)) with an <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> producing <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_ester" title="Fatty acid ester">fatty acid esters</a>.</p>
<p>Biodiesel is meant to be used in standard diesel engines and is thus distinct from the vegetable and waste oils used to fuel <i>converted</i> diesel engines. Biodiesel can be used alone, or blended with petrodiesel in any proportions.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup> Biodiesel blends can also be used as <a href="/wiki/Heating_oil" title="Heating oil">heating oil</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Biodiesel_Board" title="National Biodiesel Board">National Biodiesel Board</a> (USA) also has a technical definition of "biodiesel" as a mono-alkyl ester.<sup id="cite_ref-NBB_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBB-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Blends">Blends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Blends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/170px-Biodiesel.JPG" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/255px-Biodiesel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Biodiesel.JPG/340px-Biodiesel.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="2356" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Biodiesel sample</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Blends of biodiesel and conventional hydrocarbon-based diesel are products most commonly distributed for use in the retail diesel fuel marketplace. Much of the world uses a system known as the "B" factor to state the amount of biodiesel in any fuel mix:<sup id="cite_ref-basics_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basics-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>100% biodiesel is referred to as B100</li>
<li>20% biodiesel, 80% petrodiesel is labeled B20<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li>5% biodiesel, 95% petrodiesel is labeled B5</li>
<li>2% biodiesel, 98% petrodiesel is labeled B2</li>
</ul>
<p>Blends of 20% biodiesel and lower can be used in diesel equipment with no, or only minor modifications,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> although certain manufacturers do not extend warranty coverage if equipment is damaged by these blends. The B6 to B20 blends are covered by the <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">ASTM</a> D7467 specification.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Biodiesel can also be used in its pure form (B100), but may require certain engine modifications to avoid maintenance and performance problems.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Blending B100 with petroleum diesel may be accomplished by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mixing in tanks at manufacturing point prior to delivery to tanker truck</li>
<li>Splash mixing in the tanker truck (adding specific percentages of biodiesel and petroleum diesel)</li>
<li>In-line mixing, two components arrive at tanker truck simultaneously.</li>
<li>Metered pump mixing, petroleum diesel and biodiesel meters are set to X total volume, transfer pump pulls from two points and mix is complete on leaving pump.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biodiesel can be used in pure form (B100) or may be blended with petroleum diesel at any concentration in most injection pump diesel engines. New extreme high-pressure (29,000 psi) <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> engines have strict factory limits of B5 or B20, depending on manufacturer.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Biodiesel has different <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> properties than petrodiesel, and will degrade natural <a href="/wiki/Rubber" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubber">rubber</a> <a href="/wiki/Gasket" title="Gasket">gaskets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hose_(tubing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hose (tubing)">hoses</a> in vehicles (mostly vehicles manufactured before 1992), although these tend to wear out naturally and most likely will have already been replaced with <a href="/wiki/FKM" title="FKM">FKM</a>, which is nonreactive to biodiesel. Biodiesel has been known to break down deposits of residue in the fuel lines where petrodiesel has been used.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Fuel_filter" title="Fuel filter">fuel filters</a> may become clogged with particulates if a quick transition to pure biodiesel is made. Therefore, it is recommended to change the fuel filters on engines and heaters shortly after first switching to a biodiesel blend.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act of 2005</a>, biodiesel use has been increasing in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In the UK, the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Transport_Fuel_Obligation" title="Renewable Transport Fuel Obligation">Renewable Transport Fuel Obligation</a> obliges suppliers to include 5% renewable fuel in all transport fuel sold in the UK by 2010. For road diesel, this effectively means 5% biodiesel (B5).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicular_use_and_manufacturer_acceptance">Vehicular use and manufacturer acceptance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vehicular use and manufacturer acceptance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, Chrysler (then part of DaimlerChrysler) released the Jeep Liberty CRD diesels from the factory into the European market with 5% biodiesel blends, indicating at least partial acceptance of biodiesel as an acceptable diesel fuel additive.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2007, DaimlerChrysler indicated its intention to increase warranty coverage to 20% biodiesel blends if biofuel quality in the United States can be standardized.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Group" title="Volkswagen Group">Volkswagen Group</a> has released a statement indicating that several of its vehicles are compatible with B5 and B100 made from <a href="/wiki/Rape_seed" class="mw-redirect" title="Rape seed">rape seed</a> oil and compatible with the <a href="/wiki/EN_14214" title="EN 14214">EN 14214</a> standard. The use of the specified biodiesel type in its cars will not void any warranty.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mercedes_Benz" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercedes Benz">Mercedes Benz</a> does not allow diesel fuels containing greater than 5% biodiesel (B5) due to concerns about "production shortcomings".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Any damages caused by the use of such non-approved fuels will not be covered by the Mercedes-Benz Limited Warranty.</p>
<p>Starting in 2004, the city of <a href="/wiki/Halifax_Regional_Municipality" class="mw-redirect" title="Halifax Regional Municipality">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a> decided to update its bus system to allow the fleet of city buses to run entirely on a fish-oil based biodiesel. This caused the city some initial mechanical issues, but after several years of refining, the entire fleet had successfully been converted.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, McDonald's of UK announced it would start producing biodiesel from the waste oil byproduct of its restaurants. This fuel would be used to run its fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2014 Chevy Cruze Clean Turbo Diesel, direct from the factory, will be rated for up to B20 (blend of 20% biodiesel / 80% regular diesel) biodiesel compatibility<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_usage">Railway usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Railway usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG/220px-Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG/330px-Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG/440px-Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3031" data-file-height="2021" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_locomotive_at_Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Biodiesel locomotive and its external fuel tank at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway" title="Mount Washington Cog Railway">Mount Washington Cog Railway</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>British <a href="/wiki/Train_operating_company" title="Train operating company">train operating company</a> <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Trains" title="Virgin Trains">Virgin Trains</a> claimed to have run the UK's first "biodiesel train", which was converted to run on 80% petrodiesel and 20% biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Train" title="British Royal Train">British Royal Train</a> on 15 September 2007 completed its first ever journey run on 100% biodiesel fuel supplied by Green Fuels Ltd. <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a> and Green Fuels managing director James Hygate were the first passengers on a train fueled entirely by biodiesel fuel. Since 2007, the Royal Train has operated successfully on B100 (100% biodiesel).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, a state-owned <a href="/wiki/Short-line_railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-line railroad">short-line railroad</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Washington" title="Eastern Washington">eastern Washington</a> ran a test of a 25% biodiesel / 75% petrodiesel blend during the summer of 2008, purchasing fuel from a biodiesel producer sited along the railroad tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The train will be powered by biodiesel made in part from <a href="/wiki/Canola" title="Canola">canola</a> grown in agricultural regions through which the short line runs.</p>
<p>Also in 2007, Disneyland began running the park trains on B98 (98% biodiesel). The program was discontinued in 2008 due to storage issues, but in January 2009, it was announced that the park would then be running all trains on biodiesel manufactured from its own used cooking oils. This is a change from running the trains on soy-based biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, the historic <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_Cog_Railway" title="Mount Washington Cog Railway">Mt. Washington Cog Railway</a> added the first biodiesel locomotive to its all-steam locomotive fleet. The fleet has climbed up the western slopes of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> since 1868 with a peak vertical climb of 37.4 degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 8 July 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> the then Indian Railway Minister <a href="/wiki/D.V._Sadananda_Gowda" class="mw-redirect" title="D.V. Sadananda Gowda">D.V. Sadananda Gowda</a> announced in Railway Budget that 5% bio-diesel will be used in Indian Railways' Diesel Engines.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_use">Aircraft use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Aircraft use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A test flight has been performed by a Czech jet aircraft completely powered on biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-trainer_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trainer-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel#Jet_biofuels" title="Jet fuel">Other recent jet flights using biofuel</a>, however, have been using other types of renewable fuels.</p>
<p>On November 7, 2011 <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> flew the world's first commercial aviation flight on a microbially derived biofuel using Solajet™, <a href="/wiki/Solazyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Solazyme">Solazyme</a>'s algae-derived renewable jet fuel. The Eco-skies Boeing 737-800 plane was fueled with 40 percent Solajet and 60 percent petroleum-derived jet fuel. The commercial Eco-skies flight 1403 departed from Houston's IAH airport at 10:30 and landed at Chicago's ORD airport at 13:03.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2016, the Dutch flag carrier KLM contracted AltAir Fuels to supply all KLM flights departing Los Angeles International Airport with biofuel. For the next three years the Paramount, California-based company will pump biofuel directly to the airport from their nearby refinery.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_heating_oil">As a heating oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: As a heating oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bioliquids" title="Bioliquids">Bioliquids</a></div>
<p>Biodiesel can also be used as a heating fuel in domestic and commercial boilers, a mix of <a href="/wiki/Heating_oil" title="Heating oil">heating oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> which is standardized and taxed slightly differently from diesel fuel used for transportation. Bioheat® fuel is a proprietary blend of biodiesel and traditional heating oil. Bioheat® is a registered trademark of the <a href="/wiki/National_Biodiesel_Board" title="National Biodiesel Board">National Biodiesel Board</a> [NBB] and the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nora-oilheat.org/site20/index.mv?screen=home">National Oilheat Research Alliance</a> [NORA] in the U.S., and Columbia Fuels in Canada).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Heating biodiesel is available in various blends. ASTM 396 recognizes blends of up to 5 percent biodiesel as equivalent to pure petroleum heating oil. Blends of higher levels of up to 20% biofuel are used by many consumers. Research is underway to determine whether such blends affect performance.</p>
<p>Older furnaces may contain rubber parts that would be affected by biodiesel's solvent properties, but can otherwise burn biodiesel without any conversion required. Care must be taken, however, given that varnishes left behind by petrodiesel will be released and can clog pipes- fuel filtering and prompt filter replacement is required. Another approach is to start using biodiesel as a blend, and decreasing the petroleum proportion over time can allow the varnishes to come off more gradually and be less likely to clog. Thanks to its strong solvent properties, however, the furnace is cleaned out and generally becomes more efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> A technical research paper<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> describes laboratory research and field trials project using pure biodiesel and biodiesel blends as a heating fuel in oil-fired boilers. During the Biodiesel Expo 2006 in the UK, Andrew J. Robertson presented his biodiesel heating oil research from his technical paper and suggested B20 biodiesel could reduce UK household CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by 1.5&#160;million tons per year.</p>
<p>A law passed under <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> Governor <a href="/wiki/Deval_Patrick" title="Deval Patrick">Deval Patrick</a> requires all home heating diesel in that state to be 2% biofuel by July 1, 2010, and 5% biofuel by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> New York City has passed a similar law.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleaning_oil_spills">Cleaning oil spills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cleaning oil spills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With 80–90% of oil spill costs invested in shoreline cleanup, there is a search for more efficient and cost-effective methods to extract oil spills from the shorelines.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Biodiesel has displayed its capacity to significantly dissolve crude oil, depending on the source of the fatty acids. In a laboratory setting, oiled sediments that simulated polluted shorelines were sprayed with a single coat of biodiesel and exposed to simulated tides.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Biodiesel is an effective solvent to oil due to its methyl ester component, which considerably lowers the viscosity of the crude oil. Additionally, it has a higher buoyancy than crude oil, which later aids in its removal. As a result, 80% of oil was removed from cobble and fine sand, 50% in coarse sand, and 30% in gravel. Once the oil is liberated from the shoreline, the oil-biodiesel mixture is manually removed from the water surface with skimmers. Any remaining mixture is easily broken down due to the high biodegradability of biodiesel, and the increased surface area exposure of the mixture.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel_in_generators">Biodiesel in generators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biodiesel in generators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg/170px-Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg/255px-Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg/340px-Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_rental_generator.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Biodiesel is also used in rental generators</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2001, UC Riverside installed a 6-megawatt backup power system that is entirely fueled by biodiesel. Backup diesel-fueled generators allow companies to avoid damaging blackouts of critical operations at the expense of high pollution and emission rates. By using B100, these generators were able to essentially eliminate the byproducts that result in smog, ozone, and sulfur emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The use of these generators in residential areas around schools, hospitals, and the general public result in substantial reductions in poisonous carbon monoxide and particulate matter.<sup id="cite_ref-Monyem.2C_A._2001_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monyem.2C_A._2001-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_background">Historical background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Historical background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg/160px-Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" width="160" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg/240px-Rudolf_Diesel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg/320px-Rudolf_Diesel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="994" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rudolf Diesel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">Transesterification</a> of a vegetable oil was conducted as early as 1853 by Patrick Duffy, four decades before the first <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a> became functional.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a>'s prime model, a single 10&#160;ft (3.0&#160;m) iron cylinder with a flywheel at its base, ran on its own power for the first time in <a href="/wiki/Augsburg" title="Augsburg">Augsburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, on 10 August 1893 running on nothing but <a href="/wiki/Peanut_oil" title="Peanut oil">peanut oil</a>. In remembrance of this event, 10 August has been declared "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.biodieselcommunity.org/international-biodiesel-day/">International Biodiesel Day</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is often reported that Diesel designed his engine to run on peanut oil, but this is not the case. Diesel stated in his published papers, "at the Paris Exhibition in 1900 (<a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)"><i>Exposition Universelle</i></a>) there was shown by the Otto Company a small Diesel engine, which, at the request of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabinet of France">French government</a> ran on <a href="/wiki/Arachide" class="mw-redirect" title="Arachide">arachide</a> (earth-nut or pea-nut) oil (see biodiesel), and worked so smoothly that only a few people were aware of it. The engine was constructed for using mineral oil, and was then worked on vegetable oil without any alterations being made. The French Government at the time thought of testing the applicability to power production of the Arachide, or earth-nut, which grows in considerable quantities in their <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> colonies, and can easily be cultivated there." Diesel himself later conducted related tests and appeared supportive of the idea.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In a 1912 speech Diesel said, "the use of vegetable oils for engine fuels may seem insignificant today but such oils may become, in the course of time, as important as petroleum and the <a href="/wiki/Coal-tar" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal-tar">coal-tar</a> products of the present time."</p>
<p>Despite the widespread use of petroleum-derived diesel fuels, interest in vegetable oils as fuels for internal combustion engines was reported in several countries during the 1920s and 1930s and later during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, France, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, Germany, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> were reported to have tested and used vegetable oils as diesel fuels during this time. Some operational problems were reported due to the high viscosity of vegetable oils compared to petroleum diesel fuel, which results in poor <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">atomization</a> of the fuel in the fuel spray and often leads to deposits and coking of the injectors, combustion chamber and valves. Attempts to overcome these problems included heating of the vegetable oil, blending it with petroleum-derived diesel fuel or ethanol, <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluid_catalytic_cracking" title="Fluid catalytic cracking">cracking</a> of the oils.</p>
<p>On 31 August 1937, G. Chavanne of the University of Brussels (Belgium) was granted a patent for a "Procedure for the transformation of vegetable oils for their uses as fuels" (fr. "<i>Procédé de Transformation d’Huiles Végétales en Vue de Leur Utilisation comme Carburants</i>") Belgian Patent 422,877. This patent described the alcoholysis (often referred to as transesterification) of vegetable oils using ethanol (and mentions methanol) in order to separate the fatty acids from the glycerol by replacing the glycerol with short linear alcohols. This appears to be the first account of the production of what is known as "biodiesel" today.<sup id="cite_ref-knothe_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knothe-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>More recently, in 1977, Brazilian scientist Expedito Parente invented and submitted for patent, the first industrial process for the production of biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This process is classified as biodiesel by international norms, conferring a "standardized identity and quality. No other proposed biofuel has been validated by the motor industry."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> As of 2010, Parente's company <a href="/wiki/Tecbio" class="mw-redirect" title="Tecbio">Tecbio</a> is working with <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> to certify bioquerosene (bio-kerosene), another product produced and patented by the Brazilian scientist.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research into the use of transesterified <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_oil" title="Sunflower oil">sunflower oil</a>, and refining it to diesel fuel standards, was initiated in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> in 1979. By 1983, the process for producing fuel-quality, engine-tested biodiesel was completed and published internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a> company, Gaskoks, obtained the technology from the South African Agricultural Engineers; the company erected the first biodiesel <a href="/wiki/Pilot_plant" title="Pilot plant">pilot plant</a> in November 1987, and the first industrial-scale plant in April 1989 (with a capacity of 30,000 tons of <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a> per annum).</p>
<p>Throughout the 1990s, plants were opened in many European countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, Germany and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>. France launched local production of biodiesel fuel (referred to as <i>diester</i>) from rapeseed oil, which is mixed into regular diesel fuel at a level of 5%, and into the diesel fuel used by some captive fleets (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Public_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a>) at a level of 30%. <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peugeot" title="Peugeot">Peugeot</a> and other manufacturers have certified truck engines for use with up to that level of partial biodiesel; experiments with 50% biodiesel are underway. During the same period, nations in other parts of the world also saw local production of biodiesel starting up: by 1998, the Austrian Biofuels Institute had identified 21 countries with commercial biodiesel projects. 100% biodiesel is now available at many normal service stations across Europe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biodiesel has promising lubricating properties and <a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">cetane ratings</a> compared to low sulfur diesel fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Depending on the engine, this<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> might include high pressure injection pumps, pump injectors (also called <i>unit injectors</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injector" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel injector">fuel injectors</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Biodiesel_3.jpg/220px-Biodiesel_3.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Biodiesel_3.jpg/330px-Biodiesel_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Biodiesel_3.jpg/440px-Biodiesel_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biodiesel_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Older diesel Mercedes are popular for running on biodiesel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Calorific_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Calorific value">calorific value</a> of biodiesel is about 37.27 MJ/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This is 9% lower than regular Number 2 petrodiesel. Variations in biodiesel energy density is more dependent on the feedstock used than the production process. Still, these variations are less than for petrodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It has been claimed biodiesel gives better lubricity and more complete combustion thus increasing the engine energy output and partially compensating for the higher energy density of petrodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The color of biodiesel ranges from golden to dark brown, depending on the production method. It is slightly <a href="/wiki/Miscible" class="mw-redirect" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with water, has a high <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> and low <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a>. *The <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a> of biodiesel (&gt;130&#160;°C, &gt;266&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> is significantly higher than that of petroleum diesel (64&#160;°C, 147&#160;°F) or gasoline (−45&#160;°C, −52&#160;°F). Biodiesel has a density of ~ 0.88&#160;g/cm³, higher than petrodiesel ( ~ 0.85&#160;g/cm³).</p>
<p>Biodiesel contains virtually no sulfur,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and it is often used as an additive to <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Low_Sulfur_Diesel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultra-Low Sulfur Diesel">Ultra-Low Sulfur Diesel</a> (ULSD) fuel to aid with lubrication, as the sulfur compounds in petrodiesel provide much of the lubricity.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_efficiency">Fuel efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fuel efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The power output of biodiesel depends on its blend, quality, and load conditions under which the fuel is burnt. The <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">thermal efficiency</a> for example of B100 as compared to B20 will vary due to the differing energy content of the various blends. Thermal efficiency of a fuel is based in part on fuel characteristics such as: <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Specific_density" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific density">specific density</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a>; these characteristics will change as the blends as well as the quality of biodiesel varies. The <a href="/wiki/American_Society_for_Testing_and_Materials" class="mw-redirect" title="American Society for Testing and Materials">American Society for Testing and Materials</a> has set standards in order to judge the quality of a given fuel sample.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding brake thermal efficiency one study found that B40 was superior to traditional counterpart at higher compression ratios (this higher brake thermal efficiency was recorded at compression ratios of 21:1). It was noted that, as the compression ratios increased, the efficiency of all fuel types – as well as blends being tested – increased; though it was found that a blend of B40 was the most economical at a compression ratio of 21:1 over all other blends. The study implied that this increase in efficiency was due to fuel density, viscosity, and heating values of the fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Combustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fuel systems on the modern diesel engine were not designed to accommodate biodiesel, while many heavy duty engines are able to run with biodiesel blends e.g. B20.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup> Traditional <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct injection</a> fuel systems operate at roughly 3,000 psi at the injector tip while the modern <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> fuel system operates upwards of 30,000 PSI at the injector tip. Components are designed to operate at a great temperature range, from below freezing to over 1,000 degrees Fahrenheit. Diesel fuel is expected to burn efficiently and produce as few emissions as possible. As emission standards are being introduced to diesel engines the need to control harmful emissions is being designed into the parameters of diesel engine fuel systems. The traditional inline injection system is more forgiving to poorer quality fuels as opposed to the common rail fuel system. The higher pressures and tighter tolerances of the common rail system allows for greater control over atomization and injection timing. This control of atomization as well as combustion allows for greater efficiency of modern diesel engines as well as greater control over emissions. Components within a diesel fuel system interact with the fuel in a way to ensure efficient operation of the fuel system and so the engine. If an out-of-specification fuel is introduced to a system that has specific parameters of operation, then the integrity of the overall fuel system may be compromised. Some of these parameters such as spray pattern and atomization are directly related to injection timing.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>One study found that during atomization biodiesel and its blends produced droplets were greater in diameter than the droplets produced by traditional petrodiesel. The smaller droplets were attributed to the lower viscosity and surface tension of traditional petrol. It was found that droplets at the periphery of the spray pattern were larger in diameter than the droplets at the center this was attributed to the faster pressure drop at the edge of the spray pattern; there was a proportional relationship between the droplet size and the distance from the injector tip. It was found that B100 had the greatest spray penetration, this was attributed to the greater density of B100.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Having a greater droplet size can lead to; inefficiencies in the combustion, increased emissions, and decreased horse power. In another study it was found that there is a short injection delay when injecting biodiesel. This injection delay was attributed to the greater viscosity of Biodiesel. It was noted that the higher viscosity and the greater <a href="/wiki/Cetane_rating" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetane rating">cetane rating</a> of biodiesel over traditional petrodiesel lead to poor atomization, as well as mixture penetration with air during the ignition delay period.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Another study noted that this ignition delay may aid in a decrease of <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a> emission.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions">Emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emissions are inherent to the combustion of diesel fuels that are regulated by the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (<a href="/wiki/E.P.A." class="mw-redirect" title="E.P.A.">E.P.A.</a>). As these emissions are a byproduct of the combustion process, in order to ensure E.P.A. compliance a fuel system must be capable of controlling the combustion of fuels as well as the mitigation of emissions. There are a number of new technologies being phased in to control the production of diesel emissions. The <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas_recirculation" title="Exhaust gas recirculation">exhaust gas recirculation</a> system, E.G.R., and the <a href="/wiki/Diesel_particulate_filter" title="Diesel particulate filter">diesel particulate filter</a>, D.P.F., are both designed to mitigate the production of harmful emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study performed by the <a href="/wiki/Chonbuk_National_University" title="Chonbuk National University">Chonbuk National University</a> concluded that a B30 biodiesel blend reduced <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> emissions by approximately 83% and <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> emissions by roughly 33%. <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a> emissions, however, were found to increase without the application of an E.G.R. system. The study also concluded that, with E.G.R, a B20 biodiesel blend considerably reduced the emissions of the engine.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Additionally, analysis by the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> found that biodiesel had the lowest carbon emissions of the fuels tested, those being <a href="/wiki/Ultra-low-sulfur_diesel" title="Ultra-low-sulfur diesel">ultra-low-sulfur diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, corn-based <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a>, and five types of biodiesel from varying feedstocks. Their conclusions also showed great variance in carbon emissions of biodiesel based on the feedstock used. Of <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canola" title="Canola">canola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corn_oil" title="Corn oil">corn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">used cooking oil</a>, soy showed the highest carbon emissions, while used cooking oil produced the lowest.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>While studying the effect of biodiesel on a D.P.F. it was found that though the presence of sodium and potassium carbonates aided in the catalytic conversion of ash, as the diesel particulates are catalyzed, they may congregate inside the D.P.F. and so interfere with the clearances of the filter. This may cause the filter to clog and interfere with the regeneration process.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In a study on the impact of E.G.R. rates with blends of jathropa biodiesel it was shown that there was a decrease in fuel efficiency and torque output due to the use of biodiesel on a diesel engine designed with an E.G.R. system. It was found that <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a> and <a href="/wiki/CO2" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2">CO2</a> emissions increased with an increase in exhaust gas recirculation but <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a> levels decreased. The opacity<sup class="plainlinks noprint Inline-Template" style="vertical-align:text-top;white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dispenser.homenet.org/~dispenser/cgi-bin/dab_solver.py?page=Biodiesel&amp;editintro=Template:Disambiguation_needed/editintro&amp;client=Template:Dn"><span title="Link needs disambiguation. (May 2014)">disambiguation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> level of the jathropa blends was in an acceptable range, where traditional diesel was out of acceptable standards. It was shown that a decrease in Nox emissions could be obtained with an E.G.R. system. This study showed an advantage over traditional diesel within a certain operating range of the E.G.R. system.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Currently blended biodiesel fuels (B5 and B20) are being used in many heavy-duty vehicles especially transit buses in US cities. Characterization of exhaust emissions showed significant emission reductions compared to regular diesel.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Material_compatibility">Material compatibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Material compatibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Plastics: High-density polyethylene (HDPE) is compatible but <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_chloride" title="Polyvinyl chloride">polyvinyl chloride</a> (PVC) is slowly degraded.<sup id="cite_ref-basics_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basics-5">[5]</a></sup> Polystyrene is dissolved on contact with biodiesel.</li>
<li>Metals: Biodiesel (like <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>) has an effect on copper-based materials (e.g. brass), and it also affects zinc, tin, lead, and cast iron.<sup id="cite_ref-basics_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basics-5">[5]</a></sup> Stainless steels (316 and 304) and aluminum are unaffected.</li>
<li>Rubber: Biodiesel also affects types of natural rubbers found in some older engine components. Studies have also found that fluorinated elastomers (FKM) cured with peroxide and base-metal oxides can be degraded when biodiesel loses its stability caused by oxidation. Commonly used synthetic rubbers FKM- GBL-S and FKM- GF-S found in modern vehicles were found to handle biodiesel in all conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_standards">Technical standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Technical standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_standard" title="Biodiesel standard">Biodiesel standard</a></div>
<p>Biodiesel has a number of standards for its quality including European standard <a href="/wiki/EN_14214" title="EN 14214">EN 14214</a>, <a href="/wiki/ASTM_D6751" title="ASTM D6751">ASTM International D6751</a>, and others.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_temperature_gelling">Low temperature gelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Low temperature gelling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When biodiesel is cooled below a certain point, some of the molecules aggregate and form crystals. The fuel starts to appear cloudy once the crystals become larger than one quarter of the wavelengths of visible light – this is the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_point" title="Cloud point">cloud point</a> (CP). As the fuel is cooled further these crystals become larger. The lowest temperature at which fuel can pass through a 45&#160;micrometre filter is the <a href="/wiki/Cold_filter_plugging_point" title="Cold filter plugging point">cold filter plugging point</a> (CFPP). As biodiesel is cooled further it will gel and then solidify. Within Europe, there are differences in the CFPP requirements between countries. This is reflected in the different national standards of those countries. The temperature at which pure (B100) biodiesel starts to gel varies significantly and depends upon the mix of esters and therefore the feedstock oil used to produce the biodiesel. For example, biodiesel produced from low <a href="/wiki/Erucic_acid" title="Erucic acid">erucic acid</a> varieties of canola seed (RME) starts to gel at approximately −10&#160;°C (14&#160;°F). Biodiesel produced from beef <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a> tends to gel at around 16&#160;°C (61&#160;°F) and 13&#160;°C (55&#160;°F) respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> There are a number of commercially available additives that will significantly lower the pour point and cold filter plugging point of pure biodiesel. Winter operation is also possible by blending biodiesel with other fuel oils including #2 low <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> diesel fuel and #1 diesel / <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>.</p>
<p>Another approach to facilitate the use of biodiesel in cold conditions is by employing a second fuel tank for biodiesel in addition to the standard diesel fuel tank. The second fuel tank can be <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">insulated</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heating coil</a> using <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">engine coolant</a> is run through the tank. The fuel tanks can be switched over when the fuel is sufficiently warm. A similar method can be used to operate diesel vehicles using straight vegetable oil.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination_by_water">Contamination by water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contamination by water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biodiesel may contain small but problematic quantities of water. Although it is only slightly miscible with water it is <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopy" title="Hygroscopy">hygroscopic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> One of the reasons biodiesel can absorb water is the persistence of mono and diglycerides left over from an incomplete reaction. These molecules can act as an emulsifier, allowing water to mix with the biodiesel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In addition, there may be water that is residual to processing or resulting from storage tank <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a>. The presence of water is a problem because:</p>
<ul>
<li>Water reduces the heat of fuel <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, causing smoke, harder starting, and reduced <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a>.</li>
<li>Water causes <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> of fuel system components (pumps, fuel lines, etc.)</li>
<li>Microbes in water cause the paper-element filters in the system to rot and fail, causing failure of the fuel pump due to ingestion of large particles.</li>
<li>Water freezes to form ice crystals that provide sites for <a href="/wiki/Nucleation" title="Nucleation">nucleation</a>, accelerating gelling of the fuel.</li>
<li>Water causes pitting in pistons.</li>
</ul>
<p>Previously, the amount of water contaminating biodiesel has been difficult to measure by taking samples, since water and oil separate. However, it is now possible to measure the water content using water-in-oil sensors.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Water contamination is also a potential problem when using certain chemical <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> involved in the production process, substantially reducing catalytic efficiency of base (high pH) catalysts such as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>. However, the super-critical methanol production methodology, whereby the transesterification process of oil feedstock and methanol is effectuated under high temperature and pressure, has been shown to be largely unaffected by the presence of water contamination during the production phase.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Availability_and_prices">Availability and prices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Availability and prices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodiesel around the world">Biodiesel around the world</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_prices.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diesel_prices.jpg/170px-Diesel_prices.jpg" width="170" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diesel_prices.jpg/255px-Diesel_prices.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Diesel_prices.jpg/340px-Diesel_prices.jpg 2x" data-file-width="925" data-file-height="1452" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_prices.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In some countries biodiesel is less expensive than conventional diesel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Global <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_production" title="Biodiesel production">biodiesel production</a> reached 3.8&#160;million tons in 2005. Approximately 85% of biodiesel production came from the European Union.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, in the United States, average retail (at the pump) prices, including federal and state <a href="/wiki/Fuel_tax" title="Fuel tax">fuel taxes</a>, of B2/B5 were lower than <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> diesel by about 12&#160;cents, and B20 blends were the same as petrodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> However, as part of a dramatic shift in diesel pricing, by July 2009, the US DOE was reporting average costs of B20 15&#160;cents per gallon higher than petroleum diesel ($2.69/gal vs. $2.54/gal).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> B99 and B100 generally cost more than petrodiesel except where local governments provide a tax incentive or subsidy. In the month of October 2016, Biodiesel (B20) was 2 cents lower/gallon than petrodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_production" title="Biodiesel production">Biodiesel production</a>.</div>
<p>Biodiesel is commonly produced by the transesterification of the vegetable oil or animal fat feedstock. There are several methods for carrying out this transesterification reaction including the common batch process, supercritical processes, ultrasonic methods, and even microwave methods.</p>
<p>Chemically, transesterified biodiesel comprises a mix of mono-<a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> esters of long chain <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>. The most common form uses <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> (converted to sodium methoxide) to produce <a href="/wiki/Methyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Methyl">methyl</a> esters (commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_methyl_ester" title="Fatty acid methyl ester">Fatty Acid Methyl Ester</a> – FAME) as it is the cheapest alcohol available, though <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> can be used to produce an ethyl ester (commonly referred to as Fatty Acid Ethyl Ester – FAEE) biodiesel and higher alcohols such as <a href="/wiki/Isopropanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Isopropanol">isopropanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/N-Butanol" title="N-Butanol">butanol</a> have also been used. Using alcohols of higher molecular weights improves the cold flow properties of the resulting ester, at the cost of a less efficient transesterification reaction. A <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> production process is used to convert the base oil to the desired esters. Any free fatty acids (FFAs) in the base oil are either <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">converted to soap</a> and removed from the process, or they are esterified (yielding more biodiesel) using an acidic catalyst. After this processing, unlike <a href="/wiki/Straight_vegetable_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Straight vegetable oil">straight vegetable oil</a>, biodiesel has combustion properties very similar to those of petroleum diesel, and can replace it in most current uses.</p>
<p>The methanol used in most biodiesel production processes is made using fossil fuel inputs. However, there are sources of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Recycling_International" title="Carbon Recycling International">renewable methanol</a> made using carbon dioxide or biomass as feedstock, making their production processes free of fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>A by-product of the transesterification process is the production of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>. For every 1 tonne of biodiesel that is manufactured, 100&#160;kg of glycerol are produced. Originally, there was a valuable market for the glycerol, which assisted the economics of the process as a whole. However, with the increase in global biodiesel production, the market price for this crude glycerol (containing 20% water and catalyst residues) has crashed. Research is being conducted globally to use this glycerol as a chemical building block (see <a href="/wiki/Glycerol#Chemical_intermediate" title="Glycerol">chemical intermediate</a> under Wikipedia article "<a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">Glycerol</a>"). One initiative in the UK is The Glycerol Challenge.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Usually this crude glycerol has to be purified, typically by performing vacuum distillation. This is rather energy intensive. The refined glycerol (98%+ purity) can then be utilised directly, or converted into other products. The following announcements were made in 2007: A joint venture of <a href="/wiki/Ashland_Inc." title="Ashland Inc.">Ashland Inc.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cargill" title="Cargill">Cargill</a> announced plans to make <a href="/wiki/Propylene_glycol" title="Propylene glycol">propylene glycol</a> in Europe from glycerol<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Dow Chemical">Dow Chemical</a> announced similar plans for North America.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Dow also plans to build a plant in China to make <a href="/wiki/Epichlorhydrin" class="mw-redirect" title="Epichlorhydrin">epichlorhydrin</a> from glycerol.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epichlorhydrin" class="mw-redirect" title="Epichlorhydrin">Epichlorhydrin</a> is a raw material for <a href="/wiki/Epoxy_resins" class="mw-redirect" title="Epoxy resins">epoxy resins</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_levels">Production levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Production levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodiesel around the world">Biodiesel around the world</a>.</div>
<p>In 2007, biodiesel production capacity was growing rapidly, with an average annual growth rate from 2002–06 of over 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> For the year 2006, the latest for which actual production figures could be obtained, total world biodiesel production was about 5–6&#160;million tonnes, with 4.9&#160;million tonnes processed in Europe (of which 2.7&#160;million tonnes was from Germany) and most of the rest from the USA. In 2008 production in Europe alone had risen to 7.8&#160;million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In July 2009, a duty was added to American imported biodiesel in the European Union in order to balance the competition from European, especially German producers.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The capacity for 2008 in Europe totalled 16&#160;million tonnes. This compares with a total demand for diesel in the US and Europe of approximately 490&#160;million tonnes (147&#160;billion gallons).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Total world production of vegetable oil for all purposes in 2005/06 was about 110&#160;million tonnes, with about 34&#160;million tonnes each of <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>US biodiesel production in 2011 brought the industry to a new milestone. Under the EPA Renewable Fuel Standard, targets have been implemented for the biodiesel production plants in order to monitor and document production levels in comparison to total demand. According to the year-end data released by the EPA, biodiesel production in 2011 reached more than 1 billion gallons. This production number far exceeded the 800 million gallon target set by the EPA. The projected production for 2020 is nearly 12 billion gallons.<sup id="cite_ref-biodiesel.org_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biodiesel.org-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel_feedstocks">Biodiesel feedstocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Biodiesel feedstocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>A variety of oils can be used to produce biodiesel. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Virgin oil feedstock – rapeseed and <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean oils</a> are most commonly used, soybean oil <sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup> accounting for about half of U.S. production.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> It also can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Pongamia" title="Pongamia">Pongamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thlaspi_arvense" title="Thlaspi arvense">field pennycress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a> and other crops such as <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">mustard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jojoba" title="Jojoba">jojoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a> (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_vegetable_oils#Oils_used_for_biofuel" title="List of vegetable oils">list of vegetable oils for biofuel</a> for more information);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Waste_vegetable_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste vegetable oil">Waste vegetable oil</a> (WVO);</li>
<li>Animal <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> including <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">lard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_grease" title="Yellow grease">yellow grease</a>, chicken fat,<sup id="cite_ref-ChickenFat_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChickenFat-86">[86]</a></sup> and the by-products of the production of <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Omega-3 fatty acids">Omega-3 fatty acids</a> from fish oil.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">Algae</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">can be grown</a> using waste materials such as sewage<sup id="cite_ref-Kiong_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiong-87">[87]</a></sup> and without displacing land currently used for food production.</li>
<li>Oil from <a href="/wiki/Halophyte" title="Halophyte">halophytes</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Salicornia#Industrial_use_.28contemporary.29" title="Salicornia">Salicornia bigelovii</a></i>, which can be grown using saltwater in coastal areas where conventional crops cannot be grown, with yields equal to the yields of soybeans and other oilseeds grown using freshwater irrigation<sup id="cite_ref-Glenn1998_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn1998-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li>Sewage Sludge – The sewage-to-biofuel field is attracting interest from major companies like Waste Management and startups like InfoSpi, which are betting that renewable sewage biodiesel can become competitive with petroleum diesel on price.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Many advocates suggest that waste vegetable oil is the best source of oil to produce biodiesel, but since the available supply is drastically less than the amount of petroleum-based fuel that is burned for transportation and home heating in the world, this local solution could not scale to the current rate of consumption.</p>
<p>Animal fats are a by-product of meat production and cooking. Although it would not be efficient to raise animals (or catch fish) simply for their fat, use of the by-product adds value to the livestock industry (hogs, cattle, poultry). Today, multi-feedstock biodiesel facilities are producing high quality animal-fat based biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-NRA1_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRA1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AusBiofuels_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AusBiofuels-3">[3]</a></sup> Currently, a 5-million dollar plant is being built in the USA, with the intent of producing 11.4&#160;million litres (3&#160;million gallons) biodiesel from some of the estimated 1&#160;billion kg (2.2&#160;billion pounds) of chicken fat<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> produced annually at the local Tyson poultry plant.<sup id="cite_ref-ChickenFat_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChickenFat-86">[86]</a></sup> Similarly, some small-scale biodiesel factories use waste fish oil as feedstock.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> An EU-funded project (ENERFISH) suggests that at a Vietnamese plant to produce biodiesel from <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a> (basa, also known as pangasius), an output of 13 tons/day of biodiesel can be produced from 81 tons of fish waste (in turn resulting from 130 tons of fish). This project utilises the biodiesel to fuel a <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">CHP</a> unit in the fish processing plant, mainly to power the fish freezing plant.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantity_of_feedstocks_required">Quantity of feedstocks required</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Quantity of feedstocks required">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">food vs. fuel</a></div>
<p>Current worldwide production of vegetable oil and animal fat is not sufficient to replace liquid fossil fuel use. Furthermore, some object to the vast amount of farming and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> use, and land use conversion that would be needed to produce the additional vegetable oil. The estimated transportation diesel fuel and home heating oil used in the United States is about 160&#160;million tons (350&#160;billion pounds) according to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Energy">US Department of Energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In the United States, estimated production of vegetable oil for all uses is about 11&#160;million tons (24&#160;billion pounds) and estimated production of animal fat is 5.3&#160;million tonnes (12&#160;billion pounds).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the entire arable land area of the USA (470&#160;million acres, or 1.9&#160;million square kilometers) were devoted to biodiesel production from soy, this would just about provide the 160&#160;million tonnes required (assuming an optimistic 98&#160;US&#160;gal/acre of biodiesel). This land area could in principle be reduced significantly using algae, if the obstacles can be overcome. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">US DOE</a> estimates that if algae fuel replaced all the petroleum fuel in the United States, it would require 15,000 square miles (39,000 square kilometers), which is a few thousand square miles larger than <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, or 30% greater than the area of Belgium,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Briggs2004_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs2004-97">[97]</a></sup> assuming a yield of 140 tonnes/hectare (15,000&#160;US&#160;gal/acre). Given a more realistic yield of 36 tonnes/hectare (3834&#160;US&#160;gal/acre) the area required is about 152,000 square kilometers, or roughly equal to that of the state of Georgia or of England and Wales. The advantages of algae are that it can be grown on non-arable land such as deserts or in marine environments, and the potential oil yields are much higher than from plants.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yield">Yield</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Yield">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Feedstock yield efficiency per unit area affects the feasibility of ramping up production to the huge industrial levels required to power a significant percentage of vehicles.</p>
