and Chemicals", page 341. Academic Press, 1998.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kitani, Osamu, "Volume V: Energy and Biomass Engineering,<br />
CIGR Handbook of Agricultural Engineering", Amer Society of Agricultural, 1999.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Algae fuel yields have not yet been accurately determined, but DOE is reported as saying that algae yield 30 times more energy per acre than land crops such as soybeans.<sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost-algae_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost-algae-103">[99]</a></sup> Yields of 36 tonnes/hectare are considered practical by Ami Ben-Amotz of the Institute of Oceanography in <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a>, who has been farming Algae commercially for over 20&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jatropha#Vegoil_and_biodiesel" title="Jatropha">Jatropha</a> has been cited as a high-yield source of biodiesel but yields are highly dependent on climatic and soil conditions. The estimates at the low end put the yield at about 200&#160;US&#160;gal/acre (1.5-2 tonnes per hectare) per crop; in more favorable climates two or more crops per year have been achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-jatrophaex_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jatrophaex-105">[101]</a></sup> It is grown in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, is drought-resistant, and can <a href="/wiki/Intercropping" title="Intercropping">share space</a> with other cash crops such as coffee, sugar, fruits and vegetables.<sup id="cite_ref-reuk_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuk-106">[102]</a></sup> It is well-suited to semi-arid lands and can contribute to slow down <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, according to its advocates.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency_and_economic_arguments">Efficiency and economic arguments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Efficiency and economic arguments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg/170px-Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg" width="170" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg/255px-Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg/340px-Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="547" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bequer-B100-SOJA-SOYBEAM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pure biodiesel (B-100) made from soybeans</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to a study by Drs. Van Dyne and Raymer for the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley_Authority" title="Tennessee Valley Authority">Tennessee Valley Authority</a>, the average US farm consumes fuel at the rate of 82&#160;litres per hectare (8.75&#160;US&#160;gal/acre) of land to produce one crop. However, average crops of rapeseed produce oil at an average rate of 1,029&#160;L/ha (110&#160;US&#160;gal/acre), and high-yield rapeseed fields produce about 1,356&#160;L/ha (145&#160;US&#160;gal/acre). The ratio of input to output in these cases is roughly 1:12.5 and 1:16.5. Photosynthesis is known to have an <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_efficiency" title="Photosynthetic efficiency">efficiency rate</a> of about 3–6% of total solar radiation<sup id="cite_ref-www.fao.org_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.fao.org-108">[104]</a></sup> and if the entire mass of a crop is utilized for energy production, the overall efficiency of this chain is currently about 1%<sup id="cite_ref-petroleum.berkeley.edu_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-petroleum.berkeley.edu-109">[105]</a></sup> While this may compare unfavorably to <a href="/wiki/Solar_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar cells">solar cells</a> combined with an electric drive train, biodiesel is less costly to deploy (solar cells cost approximately US$250 per square meter) and transport (electric vehicles require batteries which currently have a much lower <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> than liquid fuels). A 2005 study found that biodiesel production using soybeans required 27% more fossil energy than the biodiesel produced and 118% more energy using sunflowers.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimentel_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimentel-110">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, these statistics by themselves are not enough to show whether such a change makes economic sense. Additional factors must be taken into account, such as: the fuel equivalent of the energy required for processing, the yield of fuel from raw oil, the return on cultivating food, the effect biodiesel will have on food prices and the relative cost of biodiesel versus petrodiesel, water pollution from farm run-off, soil depletion<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, and the externalized costs of political and military interference in oil-producing countries intended to control the price of petrodiesel.</p>
<p>The debate over the <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">energy balance</a> of biodiesel is ongoing. Transitioning fully to biofuels could require immense tracts of land if traditional food crops are used (although <a href="/wiki/Non_food_crop" class="mw-redirect" title="Non food crop">non food crops</a> can be utilized). The problem would be especially severe for nations with large economies, since energy consumption scales with economic output.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_and_the_Economy_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_and_the_Economy-111">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>If using only traditional food plants, most such nations do not have sufficient arable land to produce biofuel for the nation's vehicles. Nations with smaller economies (hence less energy consumption) and more arable land may be in better situations, although many regions cannot afford to divert land away from food production.</p>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Third_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Third world">third world</a> countries, biodiesel sources that use marginal land could make more sense; e.g., <a href="/wiki/Millettia_pinnata" title="Millettia pinnata">pongam oiltree</a> nuts grown along roads or <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a> grown along rail lines.<sup id="cite_ref-www.tve.org.813_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www.tve.org.813-112">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In tropical regions, such as Malaysia and Indonesia, plants that produce palm oil are being planted at a rapid pace to supply growing biodiesel demand in Europe and other markets. Scientists have shown that the removal of rainforest for palm plantations is not ecologically sound since the expansion of oil palm plantations poses a threat to natural rainforest and biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilcove_and_Koh_2010_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilcove_and_Koh_2010-113">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been estimated in Germany that palm oil biodiesel has less than one third of the production costs of rapeseed biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-Palm_Oil_Based_Biodiesel_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palm_Oil_Based_Biodiesel-114">[110]</a></sup> The direct source of the energy content of biodiesel is solar energy captured by plants during <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Regarding the positive energy balance of biodiesel<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>When straw was left in the field, biodiesel production was strongly energy positive, yielding 1 GJ biodiesel for every 0.561 GJ of energy input (a yield/cost ratio of 1.78).</dd>
<dd>When straw was burned as fuel and oilseed rapemeal was used as a fertilizer, the yield/cost ratio for biodiesel production was even better (3.71). In other words, for every unit of energy input to produce biodiesel, the output was 3.71 units (the difference of 2.71 units would be from solar energy).</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economic impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Multiple economic studies have been performed regarding the economic impact of biodiesel production. One study, commissioned by the National Biodiesel Board, reported the 2011 production of biodiesel supported 39,027 jobs and more than $2.1 billion in household income.<sup id="cite_ref-biodiesel.org_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biodiesel.org-84">[84]</a></sup> The growth in biodiesel also helps significantly increase GDP. In 2011, biodiesel created more than $3 billion in GDP. Judging by the continued growth in the Renewable Fuel Standard and the extension of the biodiesel tax incentive, the number of jobs can increase to 50,725, $2.7 billion in income, and reaching $5 billion in GDP by 2012 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-NBBEPA_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBBEPA-115">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_security">Energy security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Energy security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One of the main drivers for adoption of biodiesel is energy security. This means that a nation's dependence on oil is reduced, and substituted with use of locally available sources, such as coal, gas, or renewable sources. Thus a country can benefit from adoption of biofuels, without a reduction in greenhouse gas emissions. While the total energy balance is debated, it is clear that the dependence on oil is reduced. One example is the energy used to manufacture fertilizers, which could come from a variety of sources other than petroleum. The US National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) states that energy security is the number one driving force behind the US biofuels programme,<sup id="cite_ref-NREL_biodiesel_algae_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NREL_biodiesel_algae-116">[112]</a></sup> and a White House "Energy Security for the 21st Century" paper makes it clear that energy security is a major reason for promoting biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup> The former EU commission president, Jose Manuel Barroso, speaking at a recent EU biofuels conference, stressed that properly managed biofuels have the potential to reinforce the EU's security of supply through diversification of energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_biofuel_policies">Global biofuel policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Global biofuel policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many countries around the world are involved in the growing use and production of biofuels, such as biodiesel, as an alternative energy source to fossil fuels and oil. To foster the biofuel industry, governments have implemented legislations and laws as incentives to reduce oil dependency and to increase the use of renewable energies.<sup id="cite_ref-Sorda.2C_G._2010_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sorda.2C_G._2010-119">[115]</a></sup> Many countries have their own independent policies regarding the taxation and rebate of biodiesel use, import, and production.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was required by the Canadian Environmental Protection Act Bill C-33 that by the year 2010, gasoline contained 5% renewable content and that by 2013, diesel and heating oil contained 2% renewable content.<sup id="cite_ref-Sorda.2C_G._2010_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sorda.2C_G._2010-119">[115]</a></sup> The EcoENERGY for Biofuels Program subsidized the production of biodiesel, among other biofuels, via an incentive rate of CAN$0.20 per liter from 2008 to 2010. A decrease of $0.04 will be applied every year following, until the incentive rate reaches $0.06 in 2016. Individual provinces also have specific legislative measures in regards to biofuel use and production.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_biofuel_policies#Internal_Revenue_Service_.28IRS.29" title="United States biofuel policies">Volumetric Ethanol Excise Tax Credit</a> (VEETC) was the main source of financial support for biofuels, but was scheduled to expire in 2010. Through this act, biodiesel production guaranteed a tax credit of US$1 per gallon produced from virgin oils, and $0.50 per gallon made from recycled oils.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup> Currently soybean oil is being used to produce soybean biodiesel for many commercial purposes such as blending fuel for transportation sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The European Union is the greatest producer of biodiesel, with France and Germany being the top producers. To increase the use of biodiesel, there exist policies requiring the blending of biodiesel into fuels, including penalties if those rates are not reached. In France, the goal was to reach 10% integration but plans for that stopped in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Sorda.2C_G._2010_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sorda.2C_G._2010-119">[115]</a></sup> As an incentive for the European Union countries to continue the production of the biofuel, there are tax rebates for specific quotas of biofuel produced. In Germany, the minimum percentage of biodiesel in transport diesel is set at 7% so called "B7".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_biodiesel" title="Environmental impact of biodiesel">Environmental impact of biodiesel</a></div>
<p>The surge of interest in biodiesels has highlighted a number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_issues" title="List of environmental issues">environmental effects</a> associated with its use. These potentially include reductions in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> and the rate of <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a>.</p>
<p>According to the EPA's <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.epa.gov/otaq/renewablefuels/420r10006.pdf">Renewable Fuel Standards Program Regulatory Impact Analysis</a>, released in February 2010, biodiesel from soy oil results, on average, in a 57% reduction in greenhouse gases compared to petroleum diesel, and biodiesel produced from waste grease results in an 86% reduction. See chapter 2.6 of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.epa.gov/otaq/renewablefuels/420r10006.pdf">the EPA report</a> for more detailed information.</p>
<p>However, environmental organizations, for example, <a href="/wiki/Rettet_den_Regenwald" title="Rettet den Regenwald">Rainforest Rescue</a><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup> criticize the cultivation of plants used for biodiesel production, e.g., oil palms, soybeans and sugar cane. They say the deforestation of rainforests exacerbates climate change and that sensitive ecosystems are destroyed to clear land for oil palm, soybean and sugar cane plantations. Moreover, that biofuels contribute to world hunger, seeing as arable land is no longer used for growing foods. The <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a>(EPA) published data in January 2012, showing that biofuels made from palm oil won’t count towards the nation’s renewable fuels mandate as they are not climate-friendly.<sup id="cite_ref-news.mongabay.com_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.mongabay.com-125">[121]</a></sup> Environmentalists welcome the conclusion because the growth of oil palm plantations has driven tropical deforestation, for example, in Indonesia and Malaysia.<sup id="cite_ref-news.mongabay.com_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.mongabay.com-125">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food.2C_land_and_water_vs._fuel">Food, land and water vs. fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Food, land and water vs. fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">Food vs. fuel</a></div>
<p>In some poor countries the rising price of vegetable oil is causing problems.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> Some propose that fuel only be made from non-edible vegetable oils such as <a href="/wiki/Camelina" title="Camelina">camelina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seashore_mallow" class="mw-redirect" title="Seashore mallow">seashore mallow</a><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup> which can thrive on marginal agricultural land where many trees and crops will not grow, or would produce only low yields.</p>
<p>Others argue that the problem is more fundamental. Farmers may switch from producing food crops to producing biofuel crops to make more money, even if the new crops are not edible.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Law_of_supply_and_demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of supply and demand">law of supply and demand</a> predicts that if fewer farmers are producing food the price of food will rise. It may take some time, as farmers can take some time to change which things they are growing, but increasing demand for <a href="/wiki/First_generation_biofuel" class="mw-redirect" title="First generation biofuel">first generation biofuels</a> is likely to result in price increases for many kinds of food. Some have pointed out that there are poor farmers and poor countries who are making more money because of the higher price of vegetable oil.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biodiesel from sea algae would not necessarily displace terrestrial land currently used for food production and new <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">algaculture</a> jobs could be created.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_research">Current research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Current research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is ongoing research into finding more suitable crops and improving oil yield. Other sources are possible including human <a href="/wiki/Fecal" class="mw-redirect" title="Fecal">fecal</a> matter, with <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> building its first "fecal sludge-fed biodiesel plant."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup> Using the current yields, vast amounts of land and fresh water would be needed to produce enough oil to completely replace fossil fuel usage. It would require twice the land area of the US to be devoted to soybean production, or two-thirds to be devoted to rapeseed production, to meet current US heating and transportation needs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Specially bred mustard varieties can produce reasonably high oil yields and are very useful in <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> with cereals, and have the added benefit that the meal leftover after the oil has been pressed out can act as an effective and biodegradable pesticide.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/NFESC" class="mw-redirect" title="NFESC">NFESC</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara,_California" title="Santa Barbara, California">Santa Barbara</a>-based Biodiesel Industries is working to develop biodiesel technologies for the US navy and military, one of the largest diesel fuel users in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>A group of Spanish developers working for a company called <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://agnux.wordpress.com/tag/ecofasa/">Ecofasa</a> announced a new biofuel made from trash. The fuel is created from general urban waste which is treated by bacteria to produce fatty acids, which can be used to make biodiesel.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another approach that does not require the use of chemical for the production involves the use of genetically modified microbes.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algal_biodiesel">Algal biodiesel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Algal biodiesel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">Algaculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algal_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Algal fuel">Algal fuel</a></div>
<p>From 1978 to 1996, the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">U.S. NREL</a> experimented with using algae as a biodiesel source in the "<a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">Aquatic Species Program</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-NREL_biodiesel_algae_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NREL_biodiesel_algae-116">[112]</a></sup> A self-published article by Michael Briggs, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Hampshire" title="University of New Hampshire">UNH</a> Biodiesel Group, offers estimates for the realistic replacement of all <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">vehicular</a> fuel with biodiesel by utilizing algae that have a natural oil content greater than 50%, which Briggs suggests can be grown on algae ponds at <a href="/wiki/Wastewater_treatment" title="Wastewater treatment">wastewater treatment</a> plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Briggs2004_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs2004-97">[97]</a></sup> This oil-rich algae can then be extracted from the system and processed into biodiesel, with the dried remainder further reprocessed to create ethanol.</p>
<p>The production of algae to harvest oil for biodiesel has not yet been undertaken on a commercial scale, but <a href="/wiki/Feasibility_study" title="Feasibility study">feasibility studies</a> have been conducted to arrive at the above yield estimate. In addition to its projected high yield, algaculture&#160;— unlike <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">crop-based</a> biofuels — does not entail a decrease in <a href="/wiki/Food_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Food production">food production</a>, since it requires neither <a href="/wiki/Farmland_(farming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Farmland (farming)">farmland</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>. Many companies are pursuing algae bio-reactors for various purposes, including scaling up biodiesel production to commercial levels.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prof. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rodrigo_E._Teixeira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rodrigo E. Teixeira (page does not exist)">Rodrigo E. Teixeira</a> from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama_in_Huntsville" title="University of Alabama in Huntsville">University of Alabama in Huntsville</a> demonstrated the extraction of biodiesel lipids from wet algae using a simple and economical reaction in <a href="/wiki/Ionic_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Ionic liquids">ionic liquids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pongamia">Pongamia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pongamia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Millettia_pinnata" title="Millettia pinnata">Millettia pinnata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pongamia_oil" title="Pongamia oil">Pongamia oil</a></div>
<p><i>Millettia pinnata</i>, also known as the Pongam Oiltree or Pongamia, is a leguminous, oilseed-bearing tree that has been identified as a candidate for non-edible vegetable oil production.</p>
<p>Pongamia plantations for biodiesel production have a two-fold environmental benefit. The trees both store carbon and produce fuel oil. Pongamia grows on marginal land not fit for food crops and does not require nitrate fertilizers. The oil producing tree has the highest yield of oil producing plant (approximately 40% by weight of the seed is oil) while growing in malnourished soils with high levels of salt. It is becoming a main focus in a number of biodiesel research organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[138]</a></sup> The main advantages of Pongamia are a higher recovery and quality of oil than other crops and no direct competition with food crops. However, growth on marginal land can lead to lower oil yields which could cause competition with food crops for better soil.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jatropha">Jatropha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Jatropha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">Jatropha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jatropha_Oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Jatropha Oil">Jatropha Oil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG/150px-Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG" width="150" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG/225px-Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG/300px-Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="4288" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jatropha_Biodiesel_-_DRDO_-_Pride_of_India_-_Exhibition_-_100th_Indian_Science_Congress_-_Kolkata_2013-01-03_2579.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jatropha Biodiesel from <a href="/wiki/Defence_Research_and_Development_Organisation" title="Defence Research and Development Organisation">DRDO</a>, India.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several groups in various sectors are conducting research on Jatropha curcas, a poisonous shrub-like tree that produces seeds considered by many to be a viable source of biodiesel feedstock oil.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup> Much of this research focuses on improving the overall per acre oil yield of Jatropha through advancements in genetics, soil science, and horticultural practices.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/SG_Biofuels" title="SG Biofuels">SG Biofuels</a>, a San Diego-based Jatropha developer, has used molecular breeding and biotechnology to produce elite hybrid seeds of Jatropha that show significant yield improvements over first generation varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SG_Biofuels" title="SG Biofuels">SG Biofuels</a> also claims that additional benefits have arisen from such strains, including improved flowering synchronicity, higher resistance to pests and disease, and increased cold weather tolerance.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plant Research International, a department of the <a href="/wiki/Wageningen_University_and_Research_Centre" title="Wageningen University and Research Centre">Wageningen University and Research Centre</a> in the Netherlands, maintains an ongoing Jatropha Evaluation Project (JEP) that examines the feasibility of large scale Jatropha cultivation through field and laboratory experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Sustainable_Energy_Farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Center for Sustainable Energy Farming">Center for Sustainable Energy Farming</a> (CfSEF) is a Los Angeles-based non-profit research organization dedicated to Jatropha research in the areas of plant science, agronomy, and horticulture. Successful exploration of these disciplines is projected to increase Jatropha farm production yields by 200–300% in the next ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Fungi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A group at the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> published a paper in September 2008, stating that they had isolated large amounts of lipids from single-celled fungi and turned it into biodiesel in an economically efficient manner. More research on this fungal species; <i><a href="/wiki/Cunninghamella" title="Cunninghamella">Cunninghamella</a></i> <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mycobank.org/Biolomics.aspx?Table=Mycobank&amp;MycoBankNr_=252812">japonica</a></i>, and others, is likely to appear in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The recent discovery of a variant of the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Gliocladium_roseum" class="mw-redirect" title="Gliocladium roseum">Gliocladium roseum</a></i> points toward the production of so-called <a href="/wiki/Myco-diesel" class="mw-redirect" title="Myco-diesel">myco-diesel</a> from cellulose. This organism was recently discovered in the rainforests of northern <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> and has the unique capability of converting cellulose into medium length hydrocarbons typically found in diesel fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel_from_used_coffee_grounds">Biodiesel from used coffee grounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Biodiesel from used coffee grounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nevada,_Reno" title="University of Nevada, Reno">University of Nevada, Reno</a>, have successfully produced biodiesel from oil derived from <a href="/wiki/Used_coffee_grounds" title="Used coffee grounds">used coffee grounds</a>. Their analysis of the used grounds showed a 10% to 15% oil content (by weight). Once the oil was extracted, it underwent conventional processing into biodiesel. It is estimated that finished biodiesel could be produced for about one US dollar per gallon. Further, it was reported that "the technique is not difficult" and that "there is so much coffee around that several hundred million gallons of biodiesel could potentially be made annually." However, even if all the coffee grounds in the world were used to make fuel, the amount produced would be less than 1 percent of the diesel used in the United States annually. “It won’t solve the world’s energy problem,” Dr. Misra said of his work.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exotic_sources">Exotic sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Exotic sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recently, <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligator</a> fat was identified as a source to produce biodiesel. Every year, about 15 million pounds of <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligator</a> fat are disposed of in landfills as a waste byproduct of the alligator meat and skin industry. Studies have shown that biodiesel produced from alligator fat is similar in composition to biodiesel created from soybeans, and is cheaper to refine since it is primarily a waste product.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel_to_hydrogen-cell_power">Biodiesel to hydrogen-cell power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Biodiesel to hydrogen-cell power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A microreactor has been developed to convert biodiesel into hydrogen steam to power fuel cells.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Steam reforming</b>, also known as <b>fossil fuel reforming</b> is a process which produces hydrogen gas from hydrocarbon fuels, most notably biodiesel due to its efficiency. A **microreactor**, or reformer, is the processing device in which water vapour reacts with the liquid fuel under high temperature and pressure. Under temperatures ranging from 700 – 1100&#160;°C, a nickel-based catalyst enables the production of carbon monoxide and hydrogen:<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Hydrocarbon + H2O ⇌ CO + 3 H2 (Highly endothermic)</i></p>
<p>Furthermore, a higher yield of hydrogen gas can be harnessed by further oxidizing carbon monoxide to produce more hydrogen and carbon dioxide:</p>
<p><i>CO + H2O → CO2 + H2 (Mildly exothermic)</i></p>
<p><b>Hydrogen fuel cells background information</b></p>
<p>Fuel cells operate similar to a battery in that electricity is harnessed from chemical reactions. The difference in fuel cells when compared to batteries is their ability to be powered by the constant flow of hydrogen found in the atmosphere. Furthermore, they produce only water as a by-product, and are virtually silent. The downside of hydrogen powered fuel cells is the high cost and dangers of storing highly combustible hydrogen under pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>One way new processors can overcome the dangers of transporting hydrogen is to produce it as necessary. The microreactors can be joined to create a system that heats the hydrocarbon under high pressure to generate hydrogen gas and carbon dioxide, a process called steam reforming. This produces up to 160 gallons of hydrogen/minute and gives the potential of powering hydrogen refueling stations, or even an on-board hydrogen fuel source for hydrogen cell vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup> Implementation into cars would allow energy-rich fuels, such as biodiesel, to be transferred to kinetic energy while avoiding combustion and pollutant byproducts. The hand-sized square piece of metal contains microscopic channels with catalytic sites, which continuously convert biodiesel, and even its glycerol byproduct, to hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concerns">Concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">Issues relating to biofuels</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engine_wear">Engine wear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Engine wear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lubricity of fuel plays an important role in wear that occurs in an engine. An engine relies on its fuel to provide lubricity for the metal components that are constantly in contact with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Fazal.2C_M._A._2011_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fazal.2C_M._A._2011-157">[153]</a></sup> Biodiesel is a much better lubricant compared with petroleum diesel due to the presence of esters. Tests have shown that the addition of a small amount of biodiesel to diesel can significantly increase the lubricity of the fuel in short term.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[154]</a></sup> However, over a longer period of time (2–4 years), studies show that biodiesel loses its lubricity.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup> This could be because of enhanced corrosion over time due to oxidation of the unsaturated molecules or increased water content in biodiesel from moisture absorption.<sup id="cite_ref-Monyem.2C_A._2001_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monyem.2C_A._2001-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_viscosity">Fuel viscosity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Fuel viscosity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the main concerns regarding biodiesel is its viscosity. The viscosity of diesel is 2.5–3.2 cSt at 40&#160;°C and the viscosity of biodiesel made from soybean oil is between 4.2 and 4.6 cSt<sup id="cite_ref-Tat.2C_M.E._1999_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tat.2C_M.E._1999-160">[156]</a></sup> The viscosity of diesel must be high enough to provide sufficient lubrication for the engine parts but low enough to flow at operational temperature. High viscosity can plug the fuel filter and injection system in engines.<sup id="cite_ref-Tat.2C_M.E._1999_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tat.2C_M.E._1999-160">[156]</a></sup> Vegetable oil is composed of lipids with long chains of hydrocarbons, to reduce its viscosity the lipids are broken down into smaller molecules of esters. This is done by converting vegetable oil and animal fats into alkyl esters using transesterification to reduce their viscosity<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[157]</a></sup> Nevertheless, biodiesel viscosity remains higher than that of diesel, and the engine may not be able to use the fuel at low temperatures due to the slow flow through the fuel filter.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engine_performance">Engine performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Engine performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biodiesel has higher brake-specific fuel consumption compared to diesel, which means more biodiesel fuel consumption is required for the same torque. However, B20 biodiesel blend has been found to provide maximum increase in thermal efficiency, lowest brake-specific energy consumption, and lower harmful emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Monyem.2C_A._2001_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monyem.2C_A._2001-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fazal.2C_M._A._2011_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fazal.2C_M._A._2011-157">[153]</a></sup> The engine performance depends on the properties of the fuel, as well as on combustion, injector pressure and many other factors.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup> Since there are various blends of biodiesel, that may account for the contradicting reports as regards engine performance.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiesel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-159">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Clark, S.J.; Wagner, L.; Schrock, M.D.; Piennaar, P.G. Methyl and ethyl soybean esters as renewable fuels for diesel engines. JAOCS. 1984, 61, 1632–8</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Tat.2C_M.E._1999-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Tat.2C_M.E._1999_160-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tat.2C_M.E._1999_160-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Tat, M.E.; Van Gerpan, J.H. The Kinematic Viscosity of Biodiesel and its Blends with Diesel Fuel. JAOCS. 1999, 76, 1511–1513</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Altin, R.; Cetinkaya, S.; Yucesu, H.S. The potential of using vegetable oil fuels as fuel for diesel engines. Energy Conversion and Management. 2001, 42, 529–538</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Schmidt, W. S. Biodiesel: Cultivating Alternative Fuels" <i>Environmental Health Perspectives</i> 2007, 115, 87–91</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Knothe, G. Biodiesel and renewable diesel: A comparison. Process in energy and Combustion Science. 2010, 36, 364–373</span></li>
</ol>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group"><a href="/wiki/Engine_configuration" title="Engine configuration">Engine configuration</a><br />
<span style="font-weight:normal;">(<a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion</a>)</span></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
